					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Russian state-sponsored cyber espionage group</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"STRONTIUM" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Strontium_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Strontium (disambiguation)">Strontium (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1257001546">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme) div:not(.notheme){background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media(min-width:640px){body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table{display:table!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>caption{display:table-caption!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>tbody{display:table-row-group}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table tr{display:table-row!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table th,body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table td{padding-left:inherit;padding-right:inherit}}</style><table class="infobox vcard"><caption class="infobox-title fn org">Fancy Bear</caption><tbody><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.6em;">Formation</th><td class="infobox-data note"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2004–2007<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.6em;">Purpose</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Cyberespionage" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyberespionage">Cyberespionage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyberwarfare" title="Cyberwarfare">cyberwarfare</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.6em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Region </div></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.6em;">Methods</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Zero-day_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-day (computing)">Zero-days</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spearphishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Spearphishing">spearphishing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.6em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Official language </div></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.6em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Parent organization</div></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/GRU_(Russian_Federation)" title="GRU (Russian Federation)">GRU</a><sup id="cite_ref-Esq_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esq-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-db072018_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-db072018-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wiredjul18_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiredjul18-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.6em;">Affiliations</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Cozy_Bear" title="Cozy Bear">Cozy Bear</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right:0.6em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Formerly called</div></th><td class="infobox-data nickname"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style><div class="plainlist"><ul><li>APT28</li><li>Pawn Storm</li><li>Sofacy Group</li><li>Sednit</li><li>STRONTIUM</li><li>Tsar Team</li><li>Threat Group-4127</li><li>Grizzly Steppe (when combined with <a href="/wiki/Cozy_Bear" title="Cozy Bear">Cozy Bear</a>)</li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Fancy Bear</b><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>b<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is a Russian <a href="/wiki/Cyber_spying" title="Cyber spying">cyber espionage</a> group. Cybersecurity firm <a href="/wiki/CrowdStrike" title="CrowdStrike">CrowdStrike</a> has said with a medium level of confidence that it is associated with the Russian military intelligence agency <a href="/wiki/GRU_(Russian_Federation)" title="GRU (Russian Federation)">GRU</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The UK's <a href="/wiki/Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign and Commonwealth Office">Foreign and Commonwealth Office</a><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as security firms <a href="/wiki/SecureWorks" class="mw-redirect" title="SecureWorks">SecureWorks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-SecureWorks_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SecureWorks-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/ThreatConnect" title="ThreatConnect">ThreatConnect</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ThreatConnectRussia_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ThreatConnectRussia-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mandiant" title="Mandiant">Mandiant</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FireeyeRussia_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FireeyeRussia-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> have also said the group is sponsored by the Russian government. In 2018, an indictment by the United States <a href="/wiki/Special_Counsel_investigation_(2017%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Special Counsel investigation (2017–present)">Special Counsel</a> identified Fancy Bear as <b>GRU Unit 26165</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-wiredjul18_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiredjul18-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-db072018_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-db072018-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This refers to its unified <a href="/wiki/Military_Unit_Number" title="Military Unit Number">Military Unit Number</a> of the Russian army regiments. The headquarters of Fancy Bear and the entire military unit, which reportedly specializes in state-sponsored cyberattacks and decryption of hacked data,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> were targeted by Ukrainian drones on July 24, 2023, the rooftop on an adjacent building collapsed as a result of the explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fancy Bear is classified by FireEye as an <a href="/wiki/Advanced_persistent_threat" title="Advanced persistent threat">advanced persistent threat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FireeyeRussia_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FireeyeRussia-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Among other things, it uses <a href="/wiki/Zero-day_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-day (computing)">zero-day</a> exploits, <a href="/wiki/Spear_phishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Spear phishing">spear phishing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a> to compromise targets. The group promotes the political interests of the Russian government, and is known for hacking Democratic National Committee emails to attempt to influence the outcome of the United States 2016 presidential elections.
</p><p>The name "Fancy Bear" comes from a coding system security researcher <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Alperovitch" title="Dmitri Alperovitch">Dmitri Alperovitch</a> uses to identify hackers.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Likely operating since the mid-2000s, Fancy Bear's methods are consistent with the capabilities of state actors. The group targets government, military, and security organizations, especially <a href="/wiki/Transcaucasia" class="mw-redirect" title="Transcaucasia">Transcaucasian</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>-aligned states. Fancy Bear is thought to be responsible for cyber attacks on the <a href="/wiki/Bundestag" title="Bundestag">German parliament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Storting" title="Storting">Norwegian parliament</a>, the French television station <a href="/wiki/TV5Monde" title="TV5Monde">TV5Monde</a>, the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>, NATO, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_National_Committee" title="Democratic National Committee">Democratic National Committee</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe" title="Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe">Organization for Security and Co-operation in Europe</a> and the campaign of French presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Discovery_and_security_reports">Discovery and security reports</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Discovery and security reports"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Trend_Micro" title="Trend Micro">Trend Micro</a> designated the actors behind the Sofacy malware as <b>Operation Pawn Storm</b> on October 22, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The name was due to the group's use of "two or more connected tools/tactics to attack a specific target similar to the chess strategy,"<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> known as <a href="/wiki/Pawn_storm" title="Pawn storm">pawn storm</a>.
</p><p>Network security firm <a href="/wiki/FireEye" class="mw-redirect" title="FireEye">FireEye</a> released a detailed report on Fancy Bear in October 2014. The report designated the group as "Advanced Persistent Threat 28" (APT28) and described how the <a href="/wiki/Hacker_(computer_security)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hacker (computer security)">hacking</a> group used <a href="/wiki/Zero-day_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-day (computing)">zero-day</a> exploits of the <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> operating system and <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash" title="Adobe Flash">Adobe Flash</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The report found operational details indicating that the source is a "government sponsor based in Moscow". Evidence collected by FireEye suggested that Fancy Bear's malware was compiled primarily in a <a href="/wiki/Russian-language" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian-language">Russian-language</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_Russification" title="Computer Russification">build environment</a> and occurred mainly during work hours paralleling <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Time" title="Moscow Time">Moscow's time zone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> FireEye director of threat intelligence Laura Galante referred to the group's activities as "state espionage"<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and said that targets also include "media or influencers."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The name "Fancy Bear" derives from the coding system that <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Alperovitch" title="Dmitri Alperovitch">Dmitri Alperovitch's</a> company <a href="/wiki/CrowdStrike" title="CrowdStrike">CrowdStrike</a> uses for hacker groups. "Bear" indicates that the hackers are from Russia. "Fancy" refers to "Sofacy", a word in the malware that reminded the analyst who found it, of <a href="/wiki/Iggy_Azalea" title="Iggy Azalea">Iggy Azalea</a>'s song "<a href="/wiki/Fancy_(Iggy_Azalea_song)" title="Fancy (Iggy Azalea song)">Fancy</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Esq_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esq-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Attacks">Attacks</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Attacks"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Fancy Bear's targets have included Eastern European governments and militaries, the country of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, Ukraine,<sup id="cite_ref-hitlist_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hitlist-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> security-related organizations such as <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>, as well as US defense contractors <a href="/wiki/Academi" class="mw-redirect" title="Academi">Academi</a> (formerly known as Blackwater and Xe Services), <a href="/wiki/Science_Applications_International_Corporation" title="Science Applications International Corporation">Science Applications International Corporation</a> (SAIC),<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Boeing, Lockheed Martin, and Raytheon.<sup id="cite_ref-hitlist_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hitlist-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fancy Bear has also attacked citizens of the Russian Federation that are political enemies of the Kremlin, including former oil tycoon <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Khodorkovsky" title="Mikhail Khodorkovsky">Mikhail Khodorkovsky</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maria_Alekhina" class="mw-redirect" title="Maria Alekhina">Maria Alekhina</a> of the band <a href="/wiki/Pussy_Riot" title="Pussy Riot">Pussy Riot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hitlist_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hitlist-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> SecureWorks, a cybersecurity firm headquartered in the United States, concluded that from March 2015 to May 2016, the "Fancy Bear" target list included not merely the United States Democratic National Committee and the Republican National Committee as well,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but tens of thousands of foes of Putin and the Kremlin in the United States, Ukraine, Russia, Georgia, and Syria. Only a handful of Republicans were targeted, however.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An AP analysis of 4,700 email accounts that had been attacked by Fancy Bear concluded that no country other than Russia would be interested in hacking so many very different targets that seemed to have nothing else in common other than their being of interest to the Russian government.<sup id="cite_ref-hitlist_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hitlist-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fancy Bear also seems to try to influence political events in order for friends or allies of the Russian government to gain power.
</p><p>In 2011–2012, Fancy Bear's first-stage malware was the "Sofacy" or SOURFACE implant. During 2013, Fancy Bear added more tools and backdoors, including CHOPSTICK, CORESHELL, JHUHUGIT, and ADVSTORESHELL.<sup id="cite_ref-GReAT_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GReAT-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Attacks_on_journalists">Attacks on journalists</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Attacks on journalists"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>From mid-2014 until the fall of 2017, Fancy Bear targeted numerous journalists in the United States, Ukraine, Russia, Moldova, the Baltics, and other countries who had written articles about <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> and the Kremlin. According to the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> and SecureWorks, this group of journalists is the third largest group targeted by Fancy Bear after diplomatic personnel and U.S. Democrats. Fancy Bear's targeted list includes <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Chen" title="Adrian Chen">Adrian Chen</a>, the Armenian journalist Maria Titizian, <a href="/wiki/Eliot_Higgins" title="Eliot Higgins">Eliot Higgins</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bellingcat" title="Bellingcat">Bellingcat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Barry_(journalist)" title="Ellen Barry (journalist)">Ellen Barry</a> and at least 50 other <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">New York Times</a></i> reporters, at least 50 foreign correspondents based in Moscow who worked for independent news outlets, <a href="/wiki/Josh_Rogin" title="Josh Rogin">Josh Rogin</a>, a <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">Washington Post</a></i> columnist, <a href="/wiki/Shane_Harris" title="Shane Harris">Shane Harris</a>, a <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Beast" title="The Daily Beast">Daily Beast</a></i> writer who in 2015 covered intelligence issues, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Weiss_(journalist)" title="Michael Weiss (journalist)">Michael Weiss</a>, a CNN security analyst, <a href="/wiki/Jamie_Kirchick" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamie Kirchick">Jamie Kirchick</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a>, 30 media targets in Ukraine, many at the <a href="/wiki/Kyiv_Post" title="Kyiv Post">Kyiv Post</a>, reporters who covered the <a href="/wiki/Russian_military_intervention_in_Ukraine_(2014%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian military intervention in Ukraine (2014–present)">Russian-backed war in eastern Ukraine</a>, as well as in Russia where the majority of journalists targeted by the hackers worked for independent news (e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Novaya_Gazeta" title="Novaya Gazeta">Novaya Gazeta</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Vedomosti" title="Vedomosti">Vedomosti</a></i>) such as <a href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%92%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%BA%D1%83%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0,_%D0%95%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%92%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%BD%D0%B0" class="extiw" title="ru:Винокурова, Екатерина Владимировна">Ekaterina Vinokurova</a> at <a href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/Znak.com" class="extiw" title="ru:Znak.com">Znak.com</a> and mainstream Russian journalists <a href="/wiki/Tina_Kandelaki" title="Tina Kandelaki">Tina Kandelaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ksenia_Sobchak" title="Ksenia Sobchak">Ksenia Sobchak</a>, and the Russian television anchor <a href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%9B%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2,_%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%BB_%D0%90%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%B1%D0%B5%D1%80%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87" class="extiw" title="ru:Лобков, Павел Альбертович">Pavel Lobkov</a>, all of which worked for <a href="/wiki/TV_Rain" title="TV Rain">TV Rain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JournalistsTargeted_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JournalistsTargeted-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="German_attacks_(from_2014)"><span id="German_attacks_.28from_2014.29"></span>German attacks (from 2014)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: German attacks (from 2014)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Fancy Bear is thought to have been responsible for a six-month-long <a href="/wiki/Cyber-attack" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyber-attack">cyber-attack</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Bundestag" title="Bundestag">German parliament</a> that began in December 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On 5 May 2020, German federal prosecutors issued an arrest warrant for <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Sergejewitsch_Badin" class="mw-redirect" title="Dmitri Sergejewitsch Badin">Dimitri Badin</a> in relation with the attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The attack completely paralyzed the Bundestag's IT infrastructure in May 2015. To resolve the situation, the entire parliament had to be taken offline for days. IT experts estimate that a total of 16 gigabytes of data were downloaded from Parliament as part of the attack.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The group is also suspected to be behind a <a href="/wiki/Spear_phishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Spear phishing">spear phishing</a> attack in August 2016 on members of the <a href="/wiki/Bundestag" title="Bundestag">Bundestag</a> and multiple political parties such as <a href="/wiki/The_Left_(Germany)" title="The Left (Germany)">Linken</a>-faction leader <a href="/wiki/Sahra_Wagenknecht" title="Sahra Wagenknecht">Sahra Wagenknecht</a>, <a href="/wiki/Junge_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Junge Union">Junge Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_Union_of_Germany" title="Christian Democratic Union of Germany">CDU</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saarland" title="Saarland">Saarland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dw1_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dw1-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Authorities feared that sensitive information could be gathered by hackers to later manipulate the public ahead of elections such as Germany's next federal election which was due in September 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-dw1_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dw1-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="U.S._military_wives'_death_threats_(February_10,_2015)"><span id="U.S._military_wives.27_death_threats_.28February_10.2C_2015.29"></span>U.S. military wives' death threats (February 10, 2015)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: U.S. military wives&#039; death threats (February 10, 2015)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Five wives of U.S. military personnel received death threats from a hacker group calling itself "CyberCaliphate", claiming to be an Islamic State affiliate, on February 10, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CyberCaliphate_CT_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CyberCaliphate_CT-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This was later discovered to have been a <a href="/wiki/False_flag" title="False flag">false flag</a> attack by Fancy Bear, when the victims' email addresses were found to have been in the Fancy Bear phishing target list.<sup id="cite_ref-CyberCaliphate_CT_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CyberCaliphate_CT-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Russian social media trolls have also been known to hype and rumor monger the threat of potential Islamic State terror attacks on U.S. soil in order to sow fear and political tension.<sup id="cite_ref-CyberCaliphate_CT_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CyberCaliphate_CT-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="French_television_hack_(April_2015)"><span id="French_television_hack_.28April_2015.29"></span>French television hack (April 2015)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: French television hack (April 2015)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>On April 8, 2015, French television network <a href="/wiki/TV5Monde" title="TV5Monde">TV5Monde</a> was the victim of a cyber-attack by a hacker group calling itself "CyberCaliphate" and claiming to have ties to the terrorist organization <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> (ISIL). French investigators later discounted the theory that militant Islamists were behind the cyber-attack, instead suspecting the involvement of Fancy Bear.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Hackers breached the network's internal systems, possibly aided by passwords openly broadcast by TV5,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> overriding the broadcast programming of the company's 12 channels for over three hours.<sup id="cite_ref-DT_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DT-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Service was only partially restored in the early hours of the following morning and normal broadcasting services were disrupted late into April 9.<sup id="cite_ref-DT_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DT-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Various computerised internal administrative and support systems including e-mail were also still shut down or otherwise inaccessible due to the attack.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DT_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DT-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The hackers also hijacked TV5Monde's <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> pages to <a href="/wiki/Doxing" title="Doxing">post the personal information of</a> relatives of French soldiers participating in actions against ISIS, along with messages critical of President <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Hollande" title="François Hollande">François Hollande</a>, arguing that <a href="/wiki/January_2015_%C3%8Ele-de-France_attacks" title="January 2015 Île-de-France attacks">the January 2015 terrorist attacks</a> were "gifts" for his "unforgivable mistake" of partaking in conflicts that "[serve] no purpose".<sup id="cite_ref-independent-isiltv5_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-isiltv5-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DT_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DT-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The director-general of TV5Monde, Yves Bigot, later said that the attack nearly destroyed the company; if it had taken longer to restore broadcasting, satellite distribution channels would have been likely to cancel their contracts. The attack was designed to be destructive, both of equipment and of the company itself, rather than for propaganda or espionage, as had been the case for most other cyber-attacks. The attack was carefully planned; the first known penetration of the network was on January 23, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Comae_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comae-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The attackers then carried out reconnaissance of TV5Monde to understand how it broadcast its signals, and constructed bespoke malicious software to corrupt and destroy the Internet-connected hardware that controlled the TV station's operations, such as the encoder systems. They used seven different points of entry, not all part of TV5Monde or even in France—one was a company based in the Netherlands that supplied the remote controlled cameras used in TV5's studios.<sup id="cite_ref-Comae_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comae-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Between February 16 and March 25 the attackers collected data on TV5 internal platforms, including its IT Internal <a href="/wiki/Wiki" title="Wiki">Wiki</a>, and verified that login credentials were still valid.<sup id="cite_ref-Comae_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comae-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the attack, the hackers ran a series of commands extracted from <a href="/wiki/TACACS" title="TACACS">TACACS</a> logs to erase the <a href="/wiki/Firmware" title="Firmware">firmware</a> from switches and <a href="/wiki/Router_(computing)" title="Router (computing)">routers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Comae_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comae-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Although the attack purported to be from IS, France's cyber-agency told Bigot to say only that the messages <i>claimed to be</i> from IS. He was later told that evidence had been found that the attackers were the APT 28 group of Russian hackers. No reason was found for the targeting of TV5Monde, and the source of the order to attack, and funding for it, is not known. It has been speculated that it was probably an attempt to test forms of cyber-weaponry. The cost was estimated at €5m ($5.6m; £4.5m) in the first year, followed by a recurring annual cost of over €3m ($3.4m; £2.7m) for new protection. The company's way of working had to change, with authentication of email, checking of flash drives before insertion, and so on, at significant detriment to efficiency for a news media company that must move information.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="root9B_report_(May_2015)"><span id="root9B_report_.28May_2015.29"></span>root9B report (May 2015)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: root9B report (May 2015)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Security firm <a href="/w/index.php?title=Root9B&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Root9B (page does not exist)">root9B</a> released a report on Fancy Bear in May 2015 announcing its discovery of a targeted spear phishing attack aimed at financial institutions. The report listed international banking institutions that were targeted, including the <a href="/wiki/United_Bank_for_Africa" title="United Bank for Africa">United Bank for Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America" title="Bank of America">Bank of America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toronto-Dominion_Bank" title="Toronto-Dominion Bank">TD Bank</a>, and UAE Bank. According to the root9B, preparations for the attacks started in June 2014 and the malware used "bore specific signatures that have historically been unique to only one organization, Sofacy."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Security journalist <a href="/wiki/Brian_Krebs" title="Brian Krebs">Brian Krebs</a> questioned the accuracy of root9B's claims, postulating that the attacks had actually originated from Nigerian phishers.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In June 2015 well respected security researcher Claudio Guarnieri published a report based on his own investigation of a concurrent SOFACY attributed exploit against the German Bundestag<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and credited root9B with having reported, "the same IP address used as <a href="/wiki/Command_and_control_(malware)" class="mw-redirect" title="Command and control (malware)">Command &amp; Control</a> server in the attack against Bundestag (176.31.112.10)", and went on to say that based on his examination of the Bundestag attack, "at least some" indicators contained within root9B's report appeared accurate, including a comparison of the hash of the malware sample from both incidents. root9B later published a technical report comparing Claudio's analysis of SOFACY attributed malware to their own sample, adding to the veracity of their original report.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="EFF_spoof,_White_House_and_NATO_attack_(August_2015)"><span id="EFF_spoof.2C_White_House_and_NATO_attack_.28August_2015.29"></span>EFF spoof, White House and NATO attack (August 2015)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: EFF spoof, White House and NATO attack (August 2015)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In August 2015, Fancy Bear used a zero-day exploit of <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spoofing_attack" title="Spoofing attack">spoofing</a> the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">Electronic Frontier Foundation</a> and launched attacks on the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>. The hackers used a spear phishing attack, directing emails to the false URL electronicfrontierfoundation.org.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="World_Anti-Doping_Agency_(August_2016)"><span id="World_Anti-Doping_Agency_.28August_2016.29"></span>World Anti-Doping Agency (August 2016)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: World Anti-Doping Agency (August 2016)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In August 2016, the <a href="/wiki/World_Anti-Doping_Agency" title="World Anti-Doping Agency">World Anti-Doping Agency</a> reported the receipt of <a href="/wiki/Phishing" title="Phishing">phishing</a> emails sent to users of its database claiming to be official WADA communications requesting their login details. After reviewing the two domains provided by WADA, it was found that the websites' registration and hosting information were consistent with the Russian hacking group Fancy Bear.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to WADA, some of the data the hackers released had been forged.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Due to evidence of widespread <a href="/wiki/Doping_in_Russia" title="Doping in Russia">doping by Russian athletes</a>, WADA recommended that Russian athletes be barred from participating in the 2016 Rio Olympics and Paralympics. Analysts said they believed the hack was in part an act of retaliation against whistleblowing Russian athlete <a href="/wiki/Yuliya_Stepanova" title="Yuliya Stepanova">Yuliya Stepanova</a>, whose personal information was released in the breach.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In August 2016, WADA revealed that their systems had been breached, explaining that hackers from Fancy Bear had used an <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> (IOC)-created account to gain access to their Anti-doping Administration and Management System (ADAMS) database.<sup id="cite_ref-NBC_News_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NBC_News-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The hackers then used the website fancybear.net to leak what they said were the Olympic drug testing files of several athletes who had received therapeutic use exemptions, including gymnast <a href="/wiki/Simone_Biles" title="Simone Biles">Simone Biles</a>, tennis players <a href="/wiki/Venus_Williams" title="Venus Williams">Venus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serena_Williams" title="Serena Williams">Serena Williams</a> and basketball player <a href="/wiki/Elena_Delle_Donne" title="Elena Delle Donne">Elena Delle Donne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The hackers honed in on athletes who had been granted exemptions by WADA for various reasons. Subsequent leaks included athletes from many other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-NBC_News_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NBC_News-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Dutch_Safety_Board_and_Bellingcat">Dutch Safety Board and Bellingcat</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Dutch Safety Board and Bellingcat"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eliot_Higgins" title="Eliot Higgins">Eliot Higgins</a> and other journalists associated with <a href="/wiki/Bellingcat" title="Bellingcat">Bellingcat</a>, a group researching the shooting down of <a href="/wiki/Malaysia_Airlines_Flight_17" title="Malaysia Airlines Flight 17">Malaysia Airlines Flight 17</a> over Ukraine, were targeted by numerous spearphishing emails. The messages were fake Gmail security notices with <a href="/wiki/Bit.ly" class="mw-redirect" title="Bit.ly">Bit.ly</a> and TinyCC shortened URLs. According to <a href="/wiki/ThreatConnect" title="ThreatConnect">ThreatConnect</a>, some of the phishing emails had originated from servers that Fancy Bear had used in previous attacks elsewhere. Bellingcat is known for having demonstrated that Russia is culpable for the shooting down of MH17, and is frequently derided by the Russian media.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The group targeted the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Safety_Board" title="Dutch Safety Board">Dutch Safety Board</a>, the body conducting the official investigation into the crash, before and after the release of the board's final report. They set up fake SFTP and VPN servers to mimic the board's own servers, likely for the purpose of <a href="/wiki/Spearphishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Spearphishing">spearphishing</a> usernames and passwords.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A spokesman for the DSB said the attacks were not successful.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Democratic_National_Committee_(2016)"><span id="Democratic_National_Committee_.282016.29"></span>Democratic National Committee (2016)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Democratic National Committee (2016)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Fancy Bear carried out spear phishing attacks on email addresses associated with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_National_Committee" title="Democratic National Committee">Democratic National Committee</a> in the first quarter of 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AP_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On March 10, phishing emails that were mainly directed at old email addresses of 2008 Democratic campaign staffers began to arrive. One of these accounts may have yielded up to date contact lists. The next day, phishing attacks expanded to the non-public email addresses of high level Democratic Party officials. Hillaryclinton.com addresses were attacked, but required two factor authentication for access. The attack redirected towards Gmail accounts on March 19. Podesta's Gmail account was breached the same day, with 50,000 emails stolen. The phishing attacks intensified in April,<sup id="cite_ref-AP_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although the hackers seemed to become suddenly inactive for the day on April 15, which in Russia was a holiday in honor of the military's electronic warfare services.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The malware used in the attack sent stolen data to the same servers that were used for the group's 2015 attack on the <a href="/wiki/Bundestag" title="Bundestag">German parliament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Esq_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esq-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On June 14, CrowdStrike released a report publicizing the DNC hack and identifying Fancy Bear as the culprits. An online persona, <a href="/wiki/Guccifer_2.0" title="Guccifer 2.0">Guccifer 2.0</a>, then appeared, claiming sole credit for the breach.<sup id="cite_ref-Alperovitch_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alperovitch-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another sophisticated hacking group attributed to the Russian Federation, nicknamed <a href="/wiki/Cozy_Bear" title="Cozy Bear">Cozy Bear</a>, was also present in the DNC's servers at the same time. However the two groups each appeared to be unaware of the other, as each independently stole the same passwords and otherwise duplicated their efforts. Cozy Bear appears to be a different agency, one more interested in traditional long-term espionage.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A CrowdStrike forensic team determined that while Cozy Bear had been on the DNC's network for over a year, Fancy Bear had only been there a few weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Esq_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esq-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ukrainian_artillery">Ukrainian artillery</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ukrainian artillery"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Artilleryman_of_the_Afghan_National_Army.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Artilleryman_of_the_Afghan_National_Army.jpg/220px-Artilleryman_of_the_Afghan_National_Army.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Artilleryman_of_the_Afghan_National_Army.jpg/330px-Artilleryman_of_the_Afghan_National_Army.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Artilleryman_of_the_Afghan_National_Army.jpg/440px-Artilleryman_of_the_Afghan_National_Army.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1500" /></a><figcaption>An infected version of an app to control the <a href="/wiki/122_mm_howitzer_2A18_(D-30)" title="122 mm howitzer 2A18 (D-30)">D-30 Howitzer</a> was allegedly distributed to the Ukrainian artillery</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Russian_military_intervention_in_Ukraine_(2014%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian military intervention in Ukraine (2014–present)">Russian military intervention in Ukraine (2014–present)</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/CrowdStrike" title="CrowdStrike">CrowdStrike</a> from 2014 to 2016, the group used Android malware to target the Ukrainian Army's <a href="/wiki/Rocket_Forces_and_Artillery_(Ukraine)" title="Rocket Forces and Artillery (Ukraine)">Rocket Forces and Artillery</a>. They distributed an infected version of an <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">app</a> whose original purpose was to control targeting data for the <a href="/wiki/122_mm_howitzer_2A18_(D-30)" title="122 mm howitzer 2A18 (D-30)">D-30 Howitzer</a> artillery. The app, used by Ukrainian officers, was loaded with the <a href="/wiki/X_Agent_(spyware)" class="mw-redirect" title="X Agent (spyware)">X-Agent</a> spyware and posted online on military forums. CrowdStrike initially claimed that more than 80% of Ukrainian D-30 Howitzers were destroyed in the war, the highest percentage loss of any artillery pieces in the army (a percentage that had never been previously reported and would mean the loss of nearly the entire arsenal of the biggest artillery piece of the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Armed_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Ukrainian Armed Forces">Ukrainian Armed Forces</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-CrowdStrike_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrowdStrike-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_army" class="mw-redirect" title="Ukrainian army">Ukrainian army</a> CrowdStrike's numbers were incorrect and that losses in artillery weapons "were way below those reported" and that these losses "have nothing to do with the stated cause".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CrowdStrike has since revised this report after the <a href="/wiki/International_Institute_for_Strategic_Studies" title="International Institute for Strategic Studies">International Institute for Strategic Studies</a> (IISS) disavowed its original report, claiming that the malware hacks resulted in losses of 15–20% rather than their original figure of 80%.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Windows_zero-day_(October_2016)"><span id="Windows_zero-day_.28October_2016.29"></span>Windows zero-day (October 2016)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Windows zero-day (October 2016)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>On October 31, 2016, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>'s Threat Analysis Group revealed a <a href="/wiki/Zero-day_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-day (computing)">zero-day</a> vulnerability in most <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> versions that is the subject of active malware attacks. On November 1, 2016, Microsoft Executive Vice President of the Windows and Devices Group <a href="/wiki/Terry_Myerson" title="Terry Myerson">Terry Myerson</a> posted to Microsoft's Threat Research &amp; Response Blog, acknowledging the vulnerability and explaining that a "low-volume spear-phishing campaign" targeting specific users had utilized "two zero-day vulnerabilities in <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash" title="Adobe Flash">Adobe Flash</a> and the down-level Windows kernel." Microsoft pointed to Fancy Bear as the threat actor, referring to the group by their in-house code name <i>STRONTIUM</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Dutch_ministries_(February_2017)"><span id="Dutch_ministries_.28February_2017.29"></span>Dutch ministries (February 2017)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Dutch ministries (February 2017)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In February 2017, the <a href="/wiki/General_Intelligence_and_Security_Service" title="General Intelligence and Security Service">General Intelligence and Security Service</a> (AIVD) of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> revealed that Fancy Bear and Cozy Bear had made several attempts to hack into Dutch ministries, including the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_General_Affairs" title="Ministry of General Affairs">Ministry of General Affairs</a>, over the previous six months. <a href="/wiki/Rob_Bertholee" title="Rob Bertholee">Rob Bertholee</a>, head of the AIVD, said on <i><a href="/wiki/EenVandaag" title="EenVandaag">EenVandaag</a></i> that the hackers were Russian and had tried to gain access to secret government documents.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In a briefing to parliament, Dutch Minister of the Interior and Kingdom Relations <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Plasterk" title="Ronald Plasterk">Ronald Plasterk</a> announced that votes for the <a href="/wiki/2017_Dutch_general_election" title="2017 Dutch general election">Dutch general election</a> in March 2017 would be counted by hand.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="IAAF_hack_(February_2017)"><span id="IAAF_hack_.28February_2017.29"></span>IAAF hack (February 2017)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: IAAF hack (February 2017)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The officials of <a href="/wiki/International_Association_of_Athletics_Federations" class="mw-redirect" title="International Association of Athletics Federations">International Association of Athletics Federations</a> (IAAF) stated in April 2017 that its servers had been hacked by the "Fancy Bear" group. The attack was detected by cybersecurity firm Context Information Security which identified that an unauthorised remote access to IAAF's servers had taken place on February 21. IAAF stated that the hackers had accessed the <i>Therapeutic Use Exemption</i> applications, needed to use medications prohibited by WADA.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="German_and_French_elections_(2016–2017)"><span id="German_and_French_elections_.282016.E2.80.932017.29"></span>German and French elections (2016–2017)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: German and French elections (2016–2017)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/French_presidential_election,_2017" class="mw-redirect" title="French presidential election, 2017">French presidential election, 2017</a>; <a href="/wiki/German_federal_election,_2017" class="mw-redirect" title="German federal election, 2017">German federal election, 2017</a>; and <a href="/wiki/2017_Macron_e-mail_leaks" title="2017 Macron e-mail leaks">2017 Macron e-mail leaks</a></div>
<p>Researchers from <a href="/wiki/Trend_Micro" title="Trend Micro">Trend Micro</a> in 2017 released a report outlining attempts by Fancy Bear to target groups related to the election campaigns of <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angela_Merkel" title="Angela Merkel">Angela Merkel</a>. According to the report, they targeted the Macron campaign with phishing and attempting to install malware on their site. French government cybersecurity agency <a href="/wiki/Agence_nationale_de_la_s%C3%A9curit%C3%A9_des_syst%C3%A8mes_d%27information" title="Agence nationale de la sécurité des systèmes d&#39;information">ANSSI</a> confirmed these attacks took place, but could not confirm APT28's responsibility.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marine_Le_Pen" title="Marine Le Pen">Marine Le Pen</a>'s campaign does not appear to have been targeted by APT28, possibly indicating Russian preference for her campaign. Putin had previously touted the benefits to Russia if Marine Le Pen were elected.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The report says they then targeted the German <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Adenauer_Foundation" title="Konrad Adenauer Foundation">Konrad Adenauer Foundation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Ebert_Foundation" title="Friedrich Ebert Foundation">Friedrich Ebert Foundation</a>, groups that are associated with Angela Merkel's <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_Union_of_Germany" title="Christian Democratic Union of Germany">Christian Democratic Union</a> and opposition <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party</a>, respectively. Fancy Bear set up fake email servers in late 2016 to send phishing emails with links to malware.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="International_Olympic_Committee_(2018)"><span id="International_Olympic_Committee_.282018.29"></span>International Olympic Committee (2018)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: International Olympic Committee (2018)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>On January 10, 2018, the "Fancy Bears Hack Team" online persona leaked what appeared to be stolen <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> (IOC) and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Olympic_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Olympic Committee">U.S. Olympic Committee</a> emails, dated from late 2016 to early 2017, were leaked in apparent retaliation for the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Athletes_from_Russia_at_the_2018_Winter_Olympics" title="Olympic Athletes from Russia at the 2018 Winter Olympics">IOC's banning of Russian athletes from the 2018 Winter Olympics</a> as a sanction for <a href="/wiki/Doping_in_Russia" title="Doping in Russia">Russia's systematic doping program</a>. The attack resembles the earlier <a href="/wiki/World_Anti-Doping_Agency" title="World Anti-Doping Agency">World Anti-Doping Agency</a> (WADA) leaks. It is not known whether the emails are fully authentic, because of Fancy Bear's history of salting stolen emails with disinformation. The mode of attack was also not known, but was probably phishing.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Cyber Security experts have also claimed that attacks also appear to have been targeting the professional sports drug test bottling company known as the Berlinger Group.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Swedish_Sports_Confederation">Swedish Sports Confederation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Swedish Sports Confederation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Sports_Confederation" title="Swedish Sports Confederation">Swedish Sports Confederation</a> reported Fancy Bear was responsible for an attack on its computers, targeting records of athletes' doping tests.<sup id="cite_ref-sweden_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sweden-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="United_States_conservative_groups_(2018)"><span id="United_States_conservative_groups_.282018.29"></span>United States conservative groups (2018)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: United States conservative groups (2018)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The software company <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> reported in August 2018 that the group had attempted to steal data from political organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Republican_Institute" title="International Republican Institute">International Republican Institute</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Institute" title="Hudson Institute">Hudson Institute think tanks</a>. The attacks were thwarted when Microsoft security staff won control of six <a href="/wiki/Windows_domain" title="Windows domain">net domains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In its announcement Microsoft advised that "we currently have no evidence these domains were used in any successful attacks before the DCU transferred control of them, nor do we have evidence to indicate the identity of the ultimate targets of any planned attack involving these domains".<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_Ecumenical_Patriarchate_and_other_clergy_(August_2018)"><span id="The_Ecumenical_Patriarchate_and_other_clergy_.28August_2018.29"></span>The Ecumenical Patriarchate and other clergy (August 2018)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: The Ecumenical Patriarchate and other clergy (August 2018)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>According to the August 2018 report by the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a>, Fancy Bear had been for years targeting the email correspondence of the officials of the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople">Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople</a> headed by the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarch" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical Patriarch">Ecumenical Patriarch</a> <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_I_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Bartholomew I of Constantinople">Bartholomew I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bloombergclergy_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloombergclergy-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The publication appeared at a time of heightened tensions between the Ecumenical Patriarchate, the seniormost of all the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Churches</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> (the Moscow Patriarchate) over the issue of the <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">full ecclesiastical independence</a> (<a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephaly</a>) for the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Ukraine#Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Religion in Ukraine">Orthodox Church in Ukraine</a>, sought after by the Ukrainian government. The publication cited experts as saying that the grant of autocephaly to the Church in Ukraine would erode the power and prestige of the Moscow Patriarchate and would undermine its claims of transnational jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-bloombergclergy_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloombergclergy-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cyber attacks also targeted Orthodox Christians in other countries as well as Muslims, Jews and Catholics in the United States, Ummah, an umbrella group for Ukrainian Muslims, the papal nuncio in Kyiv and Yosyp Zisels, who directs Ukraine's Association of Jewish Organizations and Communities.<sup id="cite_ref-bloombergclergy_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloombergclergy-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Indictments_in_2018">Indictments in 2018</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Indictments in 2018"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:FBI_GRU_Indictment_Wanted_Poster.pdf" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/FBI_GRU_Indictment_Wanted_Poster.pdf/page1-220px-FBI_GRU_Indictment_Wanted_Poster.pdf.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="285" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/FBI_GRU_Indictment_Wanted_Poster.pdf/page1-330px-FBI_GRU_Indictment_Wanted_Poster.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/FBI_GRU_Indictment_Wanted_Poster.pdf/page1-440px-FBI_GRU_Indictment_Wanted_Poster.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1275" data-file-height="1650" /></a><figcaption>FBI wanted poster of officers indicted in connection to Fancy Bear</figcaption></figure>
<p>In October 2018, an indictment by a U.S. <a href="/wiki/Federal_grand_jury" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal grand jury">federal grand jury</a> of seven Russian men,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>c<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> all GRU officers, in relation to the attacks was unsealed. The indictment states that from December 2014 until a least May 2018, the GRU officers conspired to conduct "persistent and sophisticated computer intrusions affecting U.S. persons, corporate entities, international organizations, and their respective employees located around the world, based on their strategic interest to the Russian government."<sup id="cite_ref-Penn_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-doj7_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doj7-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The U.S. Department of Justice stated that the conspiracy, among other goals, aimed "to publicize stolen information as part of an influence and disinformation campaign designed to undermine, retaliate against, and otherwise delegitimize" the efforts of the <a href="/wiki/World_Anti-Doping_Agency" title="World Anti-Doping Agency">World Anti-Doping Agency</a>, an international anti-doping organization that had published the <a href="/wiki/McLaren_Report" title="McLaren Report">McLaren Report</a>, a report that exposed <a href="/wiki/Doping_in_Russia" title="Doping in Russia">extensive doping of Russian athletes sponsored by the Russian government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Penn_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The defendants were charged with <a href="/wiki/Computer_hacking" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer hacking">computer hacking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wire_fraud" class="mw-redirect" title="Wire fraud">wire fraud</a>, aggravated <a href="/wiki/Identity_theft" title="Identity theft">identity theft</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Money_laundering" title="Money laundering">money laundering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Penn_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penn-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="2019_think_tank_attacks">2019 think tank attacks</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: 2019 think tank attacks"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In February 2019, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> announced that it had detected spear-phishing attacks from APT28, aimed at employees of the <a href="/wiki/German_Marshall_Fund" title="German Marshall Fund">German Marshall Fund</a>, Aspen Institute Germany, and the <a href="/wiki/German_Council_on_Foreign_Relations" title="German Council on Foreign Relations">German Council on Foreign Relations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hackers from the group purportedly sent phishing e-mails to 104 email addresses across Europe in an attempt to gain access to employer credentials and infect sites with malware.<sup id="cite_ref-Tucker_2019_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker_2019-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bloomberg_2019x2_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg_2019x2-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="2019_strategic_Czech_institution">2019 strategic Czech institution</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 2019 strategic Czech institution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2020, the Czech <a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Cyber_and_Information_Security_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="National Cyber and Information Security Agency (page does not exist)">National Cyber and Information Security Agency</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://cs.wikipedia.org/wiki/N%C3%A1rodn%C3%AD_%C3%BA%C5%99ad_pro_kybernetickou_a_informa%C4%8Dn%C3%AD_bezpe%C4%8Dnost" class="extiw" title="cs:Národní úřad pro kybernetickou a informační bezpečnost">cs</a>&#93;</span> reported a cyber-espionage incident in an unnamed strategic institution, possibly the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Czech_Republic)" title="Ministry of Foreign Affairs (Czech Republic)">Ministry of Foreign Affairs</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> most likely carried out by Fancy Bear.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="2020_Norwegian_Parliament_attack">2020 Norwegian Parliament attack</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 2020 Norwegian Parliament attack"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In August 2020 the Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Storting" title="Storting">Storting</a> reported a "significant cyber attack" on their e-mail system. In September 2020, Norway's <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Norway)" title="Minister of Foreign Affairs (Norway)">foreign minister</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ine_Marie_Eriksen_S%C3%B8reide" class="mw-redirect" title="Ine Marie Eriksen Søreide">Ine Marie Eriksen Søreide</a>, accused Russia of the attack. <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Police_Security_Service" title="Norwegian Police Security Service">Norwegian Police Security Service</a> concluded in December 2020 that "The analyses show that it is likely that the operation was carried out by the cyber actor referred to in open sources as APT28 and Fancy Bear," and that "sensitive content has been extracted from some of the affected email accounts.".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Characteristics_and_techniques">Characteristics and techniques</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Characteristics and techniques"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:APT28_APT29_Techniques_-_Malware.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/APT28_APT29_Techniques_-_Malware.png/220px-APT28_APT29_Techniques_-_Malware.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/APT28_APT29_Techniques_-_Malware.png/330px-APT28_APT29_Techniques_-_Malware.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/APT28_APT29_Techniques_-_Malware.png/440px-APT28_APT29_Techniques_-_Malware.png 2x" data-file-width="1431" data-file-height="805" /></a><figcaption>Diagram showing Grizzly Steppe's (Fancy Bear and <a href="/wiki/Cozy_Bear" title="Cozy Bear">Cozy Bear</a>) process of employing <a href="/wiki/Spear_phishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Spear phishing">spear phishing</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Fancy Bear employs advanced methods consistent with the capabilities of state actors.<sup id="cite_ref-SC_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SC-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They use <a href="/wiki/Spear_phishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Spear phishing">spear phishing</a> emails, malware drop websites disguised as news sources, and <a href="/wiki/Zero-day_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-day (computing)">zero-day</a> vulnerabilities. One cybersecurity research group noted their use of six different zero-day exploits in 2015, a technical feat that would require large numbers of programmers seeking out previously unknown vulnerabilities in top-of-the-line commercial software. This is regarded as a sign that Fancy Bear is a state-run program and not a gang or a lone hacker.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian2_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One of Fancy Bear's preferred targets is web-based email services.
