A typical compromise will consist of web-based email users receiving an email urgently requesting that they change their passwords to avoid being hacked. The email will contain a link to a spoof website that is designed to mimic a real webmail interface, users will attempt to login and their credentials will be stolen. The URL is often obscured as a shortened <a href="/wiki/Bit.ly" class="mw-redirect" title="Bit.ly">bit.ly</a> link<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in order to get past <a href="/wiki/Spam_filter" class="mw-redirect" title="Spam filter">spam filters</a>. Fancy Bear sends these phishing emails primarily on Mondays and Fridays. They also send emails purportedly containing links to news items, but instead linking to malware drop sites that install <a href="/wiki/Toolkits" class="mw-redirect" title="Toolkits">toolkits</a> onto the target's computer.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian2_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fancy Bear also registers domains that resemble legitimate websites, then create a spoof of the site to steal credentials from their victims.<sup id="cite_ref-Alperovitch_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alperovitch-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fancy Bear has been known to relay its command traffic through proxy networks of victims that it has previously compromised.<sup id="cite_ref-FireEye_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FireEye-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Software that Fancy Bear has used includes ADVSTORESHELL, CHOPSTICK, JHUHUGIT, and XTunnel. Fancy Bear utilises a number of implants, including Foozer, WinIDS, <a href="/wiki/X_Agent_(spyware)" class="mw-redirect" title="X Agent (spyware)">X-Agent</a>, X-Tunnel, Sofacy, and DownRange droppers.<sup id="cite_ref-Alperovitch_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alperovitch-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Based on compile times, FireEye concluded that Fancy Bear has consistently updated their malware since 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-FireEye_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FireEye-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To avert detection, Fancy Bear returns to the environment to switch their implants, changes its <a href="/wiki/Command_and_control_(malware)" class="mw-redirect" title="Command and control (malware)">command and control</a> channels, and modifies its persistent methods.<sup id="cite_ref-SC_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SC-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The threat group implements counter-analysis techniques to <a href="/wiki/Data_masking" title="Data masking">obfuscate their code</a>. They add junk data to encoded strings, making decoding difficult without the junk removal algorithm.<sup id="cite_ref-FireEye_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FireEye-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fancy Bear takes measures to prevent <a href="/wiki/Digital_forensics" title="Digital forensics">forensic analysis</a> of its hacks, resetting the timestamps on files and periodically clearing the event logs.<sup id="cite_ref-Alperovitch_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alperovitch-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to an indictment by the United States Special Counsel, X-Agent was "developed, customized, and monitored" by GRU Lieutenant Captain Nikolay Yuryevich Kozachek.<sup id="cite_ref-db072018_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-db072018-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fancy Bear has been known to tailor implants for target environments, for instance reconfiguring them to use local email servers.<sup id="cite_ref-FireEye_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FireEye-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In August 2015, Kaspersky Lab detected and blocked a version of the ADVSTORESHELL implant that had been used to target defense contractors. An hour and a half following the block, Fancy Bear actors had compiled and delivered a new backdoor for the implant.<sup id="cite_ref-GReAT_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GReAT-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Education">Education</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Education"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Unit 26165 was involved in the design of the curriculum at several Moscow public schools, including School 1101.<sup id="cite_ref-WapoDec28_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WapoDec28-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Related_personas">Related personas</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Related personas"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Fancy Bear sometimes creates online personas to sow disinformation, deflect blame, and create plausible deniability for their activities.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Guccifer_2.0">Guccifer 2.0</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Guccifer 2.0"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Guccifer_2.0" title="Guccifer 2.0">Guccifer 2.0</a></div>
<p>An online persona that first appeared and claimed responsibility for the DNC hacks the same day the story broke that Fancy Bear was responsible.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guccifer_2.0" title="Guccifer 2.0">Guccifer 2.0</a> claims to be a <a href="/wiki/Romanians" title="Romanians">Romanian</a> hacker, but when interviewed by <i><a href="/wiki/Motherboard_(website)" class="mw-redirect" title="Motherboard (website)">Motherboard</a></i> magazine, they were asked questions in <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a> and appeared to be unable to speak the language.<sup id="cite_ref-bullshit_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bullshit-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some documents they have released appear to be forgeries cobbled together from material from previous hacks and publicly available information, then salted with disinformation.<sup id="cite_ref-bullshit_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bullshit-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Fancy_Bears'_Hack_Team"><span id="Fancy_Bears.27_Hack_Team"></span>Fancy Bears' Hack Team</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Fancy Bears&#039; Hack Team"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A website created to leak documents taken in the WADA and IAAF attacks was fronted with a brief manifesto dated September 13, 2016, proclaiming that the site is owned by "Fancy Bears' hack team", which it said is an "international hack team" who "stand for fair play and clean sport".<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The site took responsibility for hacking WADA and promised that it would provide "sensational proof of famous athletes taking doping substances", beginning with the US Olympic team, which it said "disgraced its name by tainted victories".<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> WADA said some of the documents leaked under this name were forgeries, and that data had been changed.<sup id="cite_ref-changed_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-changed-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Anonymous_Poland">Anonymous Poland</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fancy_Bear&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Anonymous Poland"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> account named "Anonymous Poland" (@anpoland) claimed responsibility for the attack on the <a href="/wiki/World_Anti-Doping_Agency" title="World Anti-Doping Agency">World Anti-Doping Agency</a><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and released data stolen from the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Arbitration_for_Sport" title="Court of Arbitration for Sport">Court of Arbitration for Sport</a>, a secondary target.<sup id="cite_ref-Softpedia_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Softpedia-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> ThreatConnect supports the view that Anonymous Poland is a <a href="/wiki/Sock_puppet_account" title="Sock puppet account">sockpuppet</a> of Fancy Bear, noting the change from a historical focus on internal politics. A screen capture video uploaded by Anonymous Poland shows an account with Polish language settings, but their browser history showed that they had made searches in Google.ru (Russia) and Google.com (US), but not in Google.pl (Poland).<sup id="cite_ref-Softpedia_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Softpedia-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to cybersecurity firm FireEye, Fancy Bear uses a suite of tools that has been frequently updated since 2007, or perhaps even 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian2_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trend_Micro" title="Trend Micro">Trend Micro</a> said they can trace the activities of Pawn Storm back to 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Also known as <b>APT28</b> (by <a href="/wiki/Mandiant" title="Mandiant">Mandiant</a>), <b>Pawn Storm</b>, <b>Sofacy Group</b> (by <a href="/wiki/Kaspersky_Lab" title="Kaspersky Lab">Kaspersky</a>), <b>Sednit</b>, <b>Tsar Team</b> (by <a href="/wiki/FireEye" class="mw-redirect" title="FireEye">FireEye</a>) and <b>STRONTIUM</b> or <b>Forest Blizzard</b> (by <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-db072018_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-db072018-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Critical_Infrastructure_Security_and_Resilience:_Theories,_Methods,_Tools_..._2019_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Critical_Infrastructure_Security_and_Resilience:_Theories,_Methods,_Tools_..._2019-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Aleksei Sergeyevich Morenets (Алексей Сергеевич Моренец), Evgenii Mikhaylovich Serebriakov (Евгений Михайлович Серебряков), Ivan Sergeyevich Yermakov (Иван Сергеевич Ермаков), Artem Andreyevich Malyshev (Артём Андреевич Малышев), Dmitriy Sergeyevich Badin (Дмитрий Сергеевич Бадин), Oleg Mikhaylovich Sotnikov (Олег Михайлович Сотников), and Alexey Valerevich Minin (Алексей Валерьевич Минин).</span>

<li id="cite_note-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-93">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFDwoskinTimberg2019" class="citation news cs1">Dwoskin, Elizabeth; Timberg, Craig (February 19, 2019). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/technology/2019/02/20/microsoft-says-it-has-found-another-russian-operation-targeting-prominent-think-tanks/">"Microsoft says it has found another Russian operation targeting prominent think tanks"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190222064245/https://www.washingtonpost.com/technology/2019/02/20/microsoft-says-it-has-found-another-russian-operation-targeting-prominent-think-tanks/">Archived</a> from the original on February 22, 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">February 22,</span> 2019</span>. <q>The "spear-phishing" attacks — in which hackers send out phony emails intended to trick people into visiting websites that look authentic but in fact enable them to infiltrate their victims' corporate computer systems — were tied to the APT28 hacking group, a unit of Russian military intelligence that interfered in the 2016 U.S. election. The group targeted more than 100 European employees of the German Marshall Fund, the Aspen Institute Germany, and the German Council on Foreign Relations, influential groups that focus on transatlantic policy issues.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Washington+Post&amp;rft.atitle=Microsoft+says+it+has+found+another+Russian+operation+targeting+prominent+think+tanks&amp;rft.date=2019-02-19&amp;rft.aulast=Dwoskin&amp;rft.aufirst=Elizabeth&amp;rft.au=Timberg%2C+Craig&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.washingtonpost.com%2Ftechnology%2F2019%2F02%2F20%2Fmicrosoft-says-it-has-found-another-russian-operation-targeting-prominent-think-tanks%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFancy+Bear" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFBurt2019" class="citation news cs1">Burt, Tom (February 20, 2019). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://blogs.microsoft.com/eupolicy/2019/02/20/accountguard-expands-to-europe/">"New steps to protect Europe from continued cyber threats"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190220173034/https://blogs.microsoft.com/eupolicy/2019/02/20/accountguard-expands-to-europe/">Archived</a> from the original on February 20, 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">February 22,</span> 2019</span>. <q>The attacks against these organizations, which we're disclosing with their permission, targeted 104 accounts belonging to organization employees located in Belgium, France, Germany, Poland, Romania, and Serbia. MSTIC continues to investigate the sources of these attacks, but we are confident that many of them originated from a group we call Strontium. The attacks occurred between September and December 2018. We quickly notified each of these organizations when we discovered they were targeted so they could take steps to secure their systems, and we took a variety of technical measures to protect customers from these attacks.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=New+steps+to+protect+Europe+from+continued+cyber+threats&amp;rft.date=2019-02-20&amp;rft.aulast=Burt&amp;rft.aufirst=Tom&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fblogs.microsoft.com%2Feupolicy%2F2019%2F02%2F20%2Faccountguard-expands-to-europe%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFancy+Bear" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
